(<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib15\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib15\">2015</a>) lensing model (<em>z</em> = 4.3). For corresponding magnifications,see Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa6573t2\">2</a>.\n<a name=\"apjaa6573t3fnb\" id=\"apjaa6573t3fnb\"></a><sup> b</sup><em>L</em><sub>FUV</sub>\u00a0\u223c\u00a00.97\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a0 <em>L</em><sub>1600 \u00c5</sub> for these galaxies.\n<a name=\"apjaa6573t3fnc\" id=\"apjaa6573t3fnc\"></a><sup> c</sup>\n<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn8.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=ELex44D99rmJcNoMdf225ZckfHs%3D\" alt=\"${f}_{\\mathrm{obscured}}={\\mathrm{SFR}}_{\\mathrm{IR}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{f}_{\\mathrm{obscured}}={\\mathrm{SFR}}_{\\mathrm{IR}}</script></span></span>/<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn9.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=ip7Ly%2F1ZpJqZlmNmzsWs4U%2FY244%3D\" alt=\"${\\mathrm{SFR}}_{\\mathrm{total}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\mathrm{SFR}}_{\\mathrm{total}}</script></span></span>.\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/838/2/137/suppdata/apjaa6573t3_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/aa6573\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573t3_lr.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=wpMeqnRkd%2Fixpp3UWM9fpg3HefI%3D\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>We also fit the de-magnified UV to the near-IR photometry using EAZY (Brammer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib6\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib6\">2008</a>) to independently determine the photometric redshift. In the right panel of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa6573f4\">4</a>, we show the redshift probability distribution for each multiple image and the average of the three. While the redshift solutions from the lensing models are lower, they are consistent within the \u00b13<em>\u03c3</em> limits of the photometric redshifts from the SED fitting. A spectroscopic redshift for this multiply imaged source will help further refine the lensing models.</p><p>The rest-frame UV spectral slope (<em>\u03b2</em>, where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn10.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=dE27UxGe301loYelJXan19ucbDI%3D\" alt=\"${f}_{\\lambda }\\propto {\\lambda }^{\\beta }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{f}_{\\lambda }\\propto {\\lambda }^{\\beta }</script></span></span>) is calculated by fitting a power law to the rest-frame photometric data between the wavelength range of 1300\u20133000 \u00c5. Prior to fitting, the lensing magnification is removed from the photometric data and we propagate the magnification uncertainty. The UV luminosity, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn11.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=bEluv2h104Qwr3G1PZznYYGyjgk%3D\" alt=\"${L}_{1600}={\\nu }_{1600}{L}_{\\nu }(1600$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{1600}={\\nu }_{1600}{L}_{\\nu }(1600</script></span></span> \u00c5), is determined using the fitted value for <em>\u03b2</em>. Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa6573t3\">3</a> lists these derived UV properties corrected for magnification for each multiple image and the average of all three.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa6573s3-4\" name=\"apjaa6573s3-4\">3.4.\u00a0Star Formation Rates</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Our AzTEC detection at 1.1 mm corresponds to a rest wavelength of &lt;220 <em>\u03bc</em>m at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a04, which probes near the peak of the infrared dust emission. At this rest wavelength, we are most sensitive to the IR luminosity and not the dust mass since we are not in the Rayleigh\u2013Jeans tail of the dust distribution (e.g., <em>\u03bb</em><sub>rest</sub> &gt; 250 <em>\u03bc</em>m, Scoville et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib51\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib51\">2016</a>). Given the observed faintness of MACS0717_Az9 at 1.1 mm, we do not expect to detected it with <em>Herschel</em> (Rawle et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib44\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib44\">2016</a>). In order to determine the total IR (8\u20131000 <em>\u03bc</em>m) luminosity, <em>L</em><sub>IR</sub>, we must extrapolate using the expected SED for this galaxy. Kirkpatrick et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib26\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib26\">2015</a>) derived representative SED templates from a sample of 343 high-redshift galaxies with extensive IR data including mid-IR spectroscopy. We fit the intrinsic 1.1 mm flux, after correcting for magnification, to the SED template for a typical high-redshift star-forming galaxy (Kirkpatrick et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib26\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib26\">2015</a>). The <em>L</em><sub>IR</sub> of each component of system 5 for the two lensing models are listed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa6573t1\">1</a>.</p><p>We use the formulas from Murphy et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib38\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib38\">2011b</a>) to calculate the SFRs (assuming a Kroupa IMF). From <em>L</em><sub>IR</sub> and <em>L</em><sub>FUV</sub>, we calculate the obscured SFR<sub>IR</sub> and unobscured SFR<sub>UV</sub>, respectively. The total SFR is then calculated by summing the IR and UV SFRs. All star formation rate values are listed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa6573t3\">3</a>. We find that even though this galaxy has an intrinsically low SFR, at least 75% of the star formation is obscured. We tested our analysis with different SFR calibrations (e.g., Calzetti <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib8\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib8\">2013</a>); the obscured fraction is only slightly lower (65%) and our conclusions are unchanged.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa6573s3-5\" name=\"apjaa6573s3-5\">3.5.\u00a0Differential Lensing</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We are assuming that the magnifications derived from the optical lensing maps also apply to the longer wavelength millimeter data. For highly magnified sources, differential lensing becomes important, where extended and compact regions of a galaxy can be magnified by different factors. Hezaveh et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib19\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib19\">2012</a>) model the effects of differential lensing for strongly lensed, dusty galaxies. They find that for moderate magnifications similar to MACS0717_Az9 (<em>\u03bc</em> \u223c 7), the distribution of flux ratios between the extended and compact regions of a galaxy peaks at 1 with a FWHM of \u223c0.25 (i.e., \u223c10% uncertainty), suggesting that differential lensing does not have a large effect.</p><p>In this paper, our main comparison is between the unobscured (UV) and obscured (IR/submm) SFRs. Dusty galaxies have been found to have similar radii of \u223c2 kpc as measured in UV and (sub)mm images, while the optical sizes that trace the stellar light are more extended (Swinbank et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib55\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib55\">2010</a>; Hodge et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib20\">2016</a>). However, the UV and (sub)mm emission is not always co-spatial and can be offset by up to 1 arcsec (e.g., Iono et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib22\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib22\">2006</a>). In order to quantify the range of magnifications that might be applicable to the millimeter emission, we explore a wider area in the non-cored magnification map at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a04.1. For a lensing magnification of 7.5, 1 arcsecond offset in the source plane corresponds to 2.7 in the image plane. Within a 2.7 arcsec diameter circle around the location of the optical lensed galaxy 5.2 (where <em>\u03bc</em> = 7.5), we find the magnification ranges from 6.0\u20139.2 with a standard deviation of 0.76. Therefore, if the UV and millimeter emission are not co-spatial and are magnified by different amounts, this would result in an additional uncertainty of \u223c10% in the magnification and intrinsic flux that we derive.</p><p>Given these two tests of the effects of differential lensing, we conservatively propagate an additional uncertainty of 10% in the lensing magnification factors, which is the best we can do until we are able to spatially resolve the dust emission with ALMA.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa6573s4\" name=\"apjaa6573s4\">4.\u00a0Discussion</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We have detected dust emission in a strongly lensed, multiply imaged galaxy at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a04. The high magnification (<em>\u03bc</em> = 7.5) predicts that MACS0717_Az9 has an intrinsic <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn12.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=ZojZpmTnA%2FF%2FZmwHlkveTT1E%2BYw%3D\" alt=\"${L}_{\\mathrm{IR}}\\lt {10}^{11}\\,{L}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{\\mathrm{IR}}\\lt {10}^{11}\\,{L}_{\\odot }</script></span></span> (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn13.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=Eblf1VWGEBiIEx7UWw2bvEIuyd4%3D\" alt=\"${\\mathrm{SFR}}_{\\mathrm{IR}}=14.6\\,{M}_{\\odot }\\,{\\mathrm{yr}}^{-1}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\mathrm{SFR}}_{\\mathrm{IR}}=14.6\\,{M}_{\\odot }\\,{\\mathrm{yr}}^{-1}</script></span></span>). Previous detections of dust in multiple images of lensed galaxies have been limited to ULIRGs at <em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a03 (Borys et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib2\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib2\">2004</a>; Sheth et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib52\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib52\">2004</a>; Kneib et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib28\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib28\">2005</a>). There are very few galaxies at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a04 at the low luminosities of MACS0717_Az9 that have been detected in dust emission (e.g., Capak et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib10\">2015</a>; Schaerer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib48\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib48\">2015</a>; Watson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib59\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib59\">2015</a>; Willott et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib60\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib60\">2015</a>), and MACS0717_Az9 provides a unique opportunity to probe the star formation and dust properties in a typical galaxy at this early epoch.</p><h3 id=\"apjaa6573s4-1\" name=\"apjaa6573s4-1\">4.1.\u00a0IRX-<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn14.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=u0B0IMJ4q2wkMotRY2xS2E8DSf0%3D\" alt=\"$\\beta $\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\beta</script></span></span>\n</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>UV surveys rely on the UV slope, <em>\u03b2</em>, and its dependence on <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn15.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=6jGYvHbBW2MYCmhgaDIZNETCRCA%3D\" alt=\"${L}_{\\mathrm{UV}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{\\mathrm{UV}}</script></span></span> to estimate the dust extinction since IR observations are typically not deep enough. The measured value of <em>\u03b2</em> for MACS0717_Az9 is high relative to the distribution of <em>\u03b2</em> found for UV-selected galaxies of similar luminosity at <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a04 (Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib4\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib4\">2012</a>: <em>\u03b2</em><sub>mean</sub> = \u22122.01, <em>\u03c3</em> = 0.27, see also Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib3\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib3\">2016</a>); this means that <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn16.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=aNs%2BPGUjuP8ROHjYvvo8eZ3LzB8%3D\" alt=\"${L}_{\\mathrm{UV}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{\\mathrm{UV}}</script></span></span> alone would underestimate the IR luminosity by an order of magnitude.</p><p>In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa6573f5\">5</a>, we plot the IRX\u2013<em>\u03b2</em> relation, which compares the ratio of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn17.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=DKDhlA7OBwZxkswf2T4%2FBX8VyTE%3D\" alt=\"${L}_{\\mathrm{IR}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{\\mathrm{IR}}</script></span></span>/<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn18.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=IUbGeVOnd4hZVD06RJDEyRkBdSI%3D\" alt=\"${L}_{\\mathrm{UV}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{\\mathrm{UV}}</script></span></span> to the UV slope <em>\u03b2</em>. The solid curve is the established relationship for local starburst galaxies (Meurer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib36\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib36\">1999</a>) where <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a02 massive UV-selected galaxies are also found (Reddy et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib45\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib45\">2012</a>). The dotted curve shows the milder dust extinction found in the SMC (Pettini et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib41\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib41\">1998</a>). Capak et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib10\">2015</a>) found UV-selected galaxies at <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a05 to be closer to this SMC dust curve (see also Murphy et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib37\">2011a</a>; Lee et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib32\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib32\">2012</a>). Recently, Bouwens et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib3\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib3\">2016</a>) found that sub-<em>L</em>* galaxies also show lower values of IRX, even below the SMC. MACS0717_Az9 is shown as the red and orange stars, which are closer to the SMC dust curve than the Meurer relation, even though the dust-obscured emission dominates the SFR.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa6573f5\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa6573f5\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573f5_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=PR3TawbYRUqk%2BH5FGnGYWx9jSSo%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 5.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 5.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573f5_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=PR3TawbYRUqk%2BH5FGnGYWx9jSSo%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 5.</strong>\u00a0IRX\u2013<em>\u03b2</em> plot showing the relations for local starburst galaxies (solid curve, Meurer et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib36\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib36\">1999</a>) and the SMC (dotted curve, Pettini et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib41\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib41\">1998</a>). We show MACS0717_Az9 for the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a04.1 and <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a04.3 lens models as the red and orange stars, respectively. Even though 75%\u201380% of the star formation is coming out in the infrared, we find that MACS0717_Az9 is consistent with the SMC dust curve, similar to the Capak et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib10\">2015</a>) <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a05 UV-selected galaxies (blue triangles).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa6573f5\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573f5_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=PR3TawbYRUqk%2BH5FGnGYWx9jSSo%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa6573f5\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573f5_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=sFPJHeDtOSLBnHzMg2MNLuIAFUk%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>A lower value in IRX\u2013<em>\u03b2</em> relative to the starburst relation is usually interpreted to suggest a lower metallicity. It may seem unusual for such a dust-obscured galaxy (75%\u201380% of star formation is obscured) to have a lower metallicity. Schneider et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib49\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib49\">2016</a>) recently found significant dust emission in a local, metal-poor dwarf galaxy. By comparing to models of chemical evolution, they conclude that dust content may depend more on the density of the interstellar medium (ISM) than the metallicity, and that in situ grain growth should be especially important in the early universe. Future observations of lines sensitive to the ISM density and metallicity such as CO, [C <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>], and HCN in MACS0717_Az9 can be used to test this idea.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa6573s4-2\" name=\"apjaa6573s4-2\">4.2.\u00a0Galaxy Star Formation Sequence</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>At a given epoch, the tight relationship between the star formation rate and stellar mass implies that most normal star-forming galaxies are undergoing steady growth (e.g., Daddi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib14\">2007</a>; Noeske et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib40\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib40\">2007</a>). The stellar mass of MACS0717_Az9 is well below the estimated knee in the stellar mass function at <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a04 (Muzzin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib39\">2013</a>). In order to determine if MACS0717_Az9 is a normal galaxy for this epoch, we compare the position of this galaxy to the estimated extrapolation of the star formation sequence. In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa6573f6\">6</a>, we plot the star formation sequence at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a03\u20134 from Tomczak et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib57\">2016</a>). The <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a05 star-forming galaxies detected in submm continuum with ALMA by Capak et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib10\">2015</a>) and the extreme submillimeter galaxy, GN20 (Pope et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib42\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib42\">2006</a>; Riechers et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib47\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib47\">2014</a>; Tan et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib56\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib56\">2014</a>), are shown for comparison.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa6573f6\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa6573f6\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573f6_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=K6nEOAdYFvuec%2FCzFtfJ%2BVXIIio%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 6.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 6.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573f6_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=K6nEOAdYFvuec%2FCzFtfJ%2BVXIIio%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 6.</strong>\u00a0The star formation sequence at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a03\u20134 from Tomczak et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib57\">2016</a>, solid circles show where the data are mass-complete while open circles are incomplete measurements); the shaded region shows the \u00b11<em>\u03c3</em> best-fit relation to these data. We show the UV (unobscured), IR (obscured), and total SFRs for MACS0717_Az9 as the red and orange symbols for <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a04.1 and <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a04.3, respectively (Table <a class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa6573t3\">3</a>). Error bars are plotted only for the total SFR points for clarity. We overplot the Capak et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib10\">2015</a>) UV-selected galaxies that are detected in the submillimeter continuum and the intrinsically luminous submillimeter galaxy GN20 (Pope et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib42\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib42\">2006</a>; Riechers et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib47\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib47\">2014</a>; Tan et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib56\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib56\">2014</a>). All measurements are based on a Chabrier or Kroupa IMF, which give similar values for SFR and stellar mass (Speagle et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib54\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib54\">2014</a>).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa6573f6\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573f6_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=K6nEOAdYFvuec%2FCzFtfJ%2BVXIIio%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa6573f6\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573f6_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=%2BEG%2BmZ9BcmhFD9mpZdil4mkoUjc%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>First, we find that MACS0717_Az9 (red and orange stars for two redshift solutions) is consistent with the estimated star formation sequence for this epoch, and that this galaxy resides in a region that is relatively unexplored in the infrared. Given the error bars on MACS0717_Az9 and the uncertainty in the star formation sequence at these low masses, we do not claim that MACS0717_Az9 is below the star formation sequence but we can confidently say that the source is not an extreme starburst galaxy like GN20.</p><p>Second, we show that even though this is a normal star-forming galaxy, its SFR is dominated by the obscured component (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn19.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=4nbZbJs1f0ELTJYtFEOKKZyJyLQ%3D\" alt=\"${\\mathrm{SFR}}_{\\mathrm{IR}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\mathrm{SFR}}_{\\mathrm{IR}}</script></span></span> is at least 75% of the total SFR). This underscores the importance of accurately including this obscured component when accounting for the global SFRD, even at these high redshifts and lower stellar masses, and stresses the need for deep and wide IR/submm surveys.</p><p>Two recent papers that surveyed the Hubble Ultra Deep Field with ALMA seem to suggest a smaller number of high-redshift galaxies detected in dust emission than expected (Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib3\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib3\">2016</a>; Dunlop et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib16\">2017</a>). Without lensing, a galaxy like MACS0717_Az9 would not have been detected at the depth of the Dunlop et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib16\">2017</a>) ALMA map. The one <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a03.5 galaxy detected in the Dunlop et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib16\">2017</a>) observations has a slightly lower stellar mass (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn20.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=TYSpVyaIyqY8oyVr3IfjPciOBXs%3D\" alt=\"$4\\times {10}^{9}\\,{M}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">4\\times {10}^{9}\\,{M}_{\\odot }</script></span></span>) and higher <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn21.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=uTXqork%2Fg7ru%2FyH9bz7prfCx2kk%3D\" alt=\"${\\mathrm{SFR}}_{\\mathrm{IR}}=37\\,{M}_{\\odot }\\,{\\mathrm{yr}}^{-1}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\mathrm{SFR}}_{\\mathrm{IR}}=37\\,{M}_{\\odot }\\,{\\mathrm{yr}}^{-1}</script></span></span> than MACS0717_Az9, but is similarly dominated by the dust-obscured star formation (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn22.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=st7Gc2oy6sAeCaou9zd%2BS2n2OI8%3D\" alt=\"${f}_{\\mathrm{obscured}}=0.94$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{f}_{\\mathrm{obscured}}=0.94</script></span></span>). The high levels of dust obscuration observed in a handful of normal galaxies at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a04 suggests that we cannot easily rule out the importance of dust emission in galaxies at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a04.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa6573s4-3\" name=\"apjaa6573s4-3\">4.3.\u00a0What if 5.2 Only Has Half the Millimeter Flux?</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>In the previous sections, we assumed that all of the millimeter flux from MACS0717_Az9 is associated with 5.2 since that is the most likely result from our statistical analysis (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa6573f3\">3</a>). Here we explore how our results are affected if only half the flux of MACS0717_Az9 is associated with 5.2. If the millimeter flux is half what we assumed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa6573t1\">1</a>, then <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn23.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=GtdtweAR3i38b6FM3QvGLj435pA%3D\" alt=\"${L}_{\\mathrm{IR}}=4.9\\times {10}^{10}\\,{L}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{\\mathrm{IR}}=4.9\\times {10}^{10}\\,{L}_{\\odot }</script></span></span>, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn24.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=GyIxccm5mzxhpXidpvcgrxciwqI%3D\" alt=\"${\\mathrm{SFR}}_{\\mathrm{IR}}=7.3\\,{M}_{\\odot }\\,{\\mathrm{yr}}^{-1}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\mathrm{SFR}}_{\\mathrm{IR}}=7.3\\,{M}_{\\odot }\\,{\\mathrm{yr}}^{-1}</script></span></span>, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn25.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=DJY%2FwWhur2xpnTZXJbcL6GNNeEk%3D\" alt=\"${\\mathrm{SFR}}_{\\mathrm{total}}\\ =11.4\\,{M}_{\\odot }\\,{\\mathrm{yr}}^{-1}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\mathrm{SFR}}_{\\mathrm{total}}\\ =11.4\\,{M}_{\\odot }\\,{\\mathrm{yr}}^{-1}</script></span></span> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn26.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=FqBckCBRW3RFRcBt%2Fcuhbdc4yf0%3D\" alt=\"${f}_{\\mathrm{obscured}}=0.64$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{f}_{\\mathrm{obscured}}=0.64</script></span></span>, assuming the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a04.1 lens model. Under this assumption, the obscured SFR is slightly lower, but the total SFR is still dominated by the dust-obscured contribution. This places MACS0717_Az9 even lower on both the star formation sequence (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa6573f6\">6</a>) and the IRX\u2013<em>\u03b2</em> plot (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa6573f5\">5</a>). Therefore, the results and implications discussed in this paper are unchanged. Future observations with ALMA will confirm how much millimeter flux is associated with this multiply imaged source.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa6573s5\" name=\"apjaa6573s5\">5.\u00a0Summary</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We have directly detected dust emission in an intrinsically lower-luminosity (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn27.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=rLw59Ay6mjhYcUeYT0B1ONQ%2FVMg%3D\" alt=\"${L}_{\\mathrm{IR}}=9.7\\times {10}^{10}\\,{L}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{\\mathrm{IR}}=9.7\\times {10}^{10}\\,{L}_{\\odot }</script></span></span>) galaxy at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a04 with AzTEC on the LMT. Currently, this is the only star-forming galaxy at such a low luminosity (sub-LIRG) where multiple images are detected in dust emission. While the SNRs of the individual images are modest, the false detection rate for randomly detecting two multiple images of a known system at the correct flux ratio given the known magnifications is negligible. We calculate the unobscured SFR from the UV and the obscured SFR from the IR and calculate a total intrinsic SFR of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn28.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=%2Fa2lp%2FucXO7peb4G3DE7Hu2Acqc%3D\" alt=\"$18.7\\,{M}_{\\odot }\\,{\\mathrm{yr}}^{-1}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">18.7\\,{M}_{\\odot }\\,{\\mathrm{yr}}^{-1}</script></span></span>, 75%\u201380% of which is obscured. MACS0717_Az9 is a normal star-forming galaxy with an intrinsic stellar mass of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn29.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=l92VmnTg9pYJ6dPjwuEOCX3HcI0%3D\" alt=\"$6.9\\times {10}^{9}\\,{M}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">6.9\\times {10}^{9}\\,{M}_{\\odot }</script></span></span> and is consistent with the estimated star formation sequence at <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a04. The dust obscuration in MACS0717_Az9 appears to be more like that of the SMC than local starburst galaxies. While we might expect lower metallicities for a lower mass galaxy 1.5 Gyrs after the Big Bang (R\u00e9my-Ruyer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib46\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib46\">2015</a>), our rest-frame IR observations find a significant dust component. Further observations to constrain the conditions of the the gas and dust in MACS0717_Az9 and future surveys with the 50 m LMT (observational limit of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573ieqn30.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=oQxAqR6IuaGPLE%2B6YoU7HIvC8yo%3D\" alt=\"${L}_{\\mathrm{IR}}\\sim {10}^{11}\\,{L}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{\\mathrm{IR}}\\sim {10}^{11}\\,{L}_{\\odot }</script></span></span>) will help constrain the buildup of metals and dust in early galaxy evolution.</p></div> <div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We thank the referee for constructive comments on this paper. This work would not have been possible without the long-term financial support from the Mexican Science and Technology Funding Agency, CONACYT during the construction and early operational phase of the Large Millimeter Telescope Alfonso Serrano, as well as support from the the US National Science Foundation (NSF) via the University Radio Observatory program, INAOE and the University of Massachusetts, Amherst. This work utilizes gravitational lensing models produced by PIs Bradac, Natarajan &amp; Kneib (CATS), Merten &amp; Zitrin, Sharon, and Williams, and the GLAFIC and Diego groups. This lens modeling was partially funded by the <em>HST</em> Frontier Fields program conducted by STScI. STScI is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy, Inc. under NASA contract NAS5-26555. The lens models were obtained from the Mikulski Archive for Space Telescopes (MAST). We also thank J. Diego for information on their lens model. This work is based in part on observations made with the <em>Spitzer Space Telescope</em>, which is operated by the Jet Propulsion Laboratory, California Institute of Technology under a contract with NASA. A.P. acknowledges valuable discussions with Sandy Faber, George Rieke and Adi Zitrin. I.A. and D.R.G. acknowledge support from CONACYT grants CB-2011-01-1672 and CB-2011-01-167281, respectively. M.L. acknowledges CNRS and CNES for support. D.M. acknowledges the support of the NSF under Grant 1513473, and from <em>HST</em>-AR-14302, provided by NASA through a grant from STScI, which is operated by AURA, under NASA contract NAS5-26555. D.L.V. acknowledges support from the NASA Keck PI Data Awards, administered by the NASA Exoplanet Science Institute. K.E.W. gratefully acknowledge support by NASA through Hubble Fellowship grant #HF2-51368 awarded by the Space Telescope Science Institute, which is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy, Inc., for NASA.</p><p><em>Facilities:</em> LMT - ,  <em>HST</em> - ,  <em>Spitzer</em> - .</p></div></div><!-- End article full text -->\n<!-- Start Footnotes -->\n\t<h2 id=\"footnotes\">Footnotes</h2>\n\t\t<div data-mobile-collapse=\"\">\n\t\t\t<ul class=\"clear-list wd-content-footnotes\">\n\t\t\t\t<li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaa6573fn1\" id=\"apjaa6573fn1\">18\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>We use \"normal\" to refer to typical star-forming galaxies for their epoch; on the star formation sequence (Noeske et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib40\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib40\">2007</a>), and/or with stellar masses near the knee of the stellar mass function (Muzzin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib39\">2013</a>).</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaa6573fn2\" id=\"apjaa6573fn2\">19\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>\n<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"http://www.stsci.edu/hst/campaigns/frontier-fields/\">http://www.stsci.edu/hst/campaigns/frontier-fields/</a>\n</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaa6573fn3\" id=\"apjaa6573fn3\">20\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>\n<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"https://archive.stsci.edu/prepds/frontier/lensmodels/\">https://archive.stsci.edu/prepds/frontier/lensmodels/</a>\n</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaa6573fn4\" id=\"apjaa6573fn4\">21\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The LMT is transitioning to a 50 m telescope in 2017.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaa6573fn5\" id=\"apjaa6573fn5\">22\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>\n<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"http://people.umass.edu/apope1/FF/\">http://people.umass.edu/apope1/FF/</a>\n</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaa6573fn6\" id=\"apjaa6573fn6\">23\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>If we instead assumed a Calzetti et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib9\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib9\">2000</a>) dust attenuation law and a range in metallicities (from sub-solar to super-solar), the stellar masses from the best-fit SEDs are slightly larger, but consistent within the uncertainties, than the values in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa6573t3\">3</a>.</p></p></div></li></ul>\n\t\t</div>\n\t<!-- End Footnotes -->\t\n<!-- Start References List -->\n\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c itemprop=\"sameAs\" href=\"https://doi.org/10.3847/1538-4357/aa6573\">https://doi.org/10.3847/1538-4357/aa6573</a></p>\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t<!-- End DOI -->\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t<!-- Start Accepted manuscript Link -->\n<!-- End Accepted manuscript Link -->\n<!-- Start Annotations -->\n <!-- End Annotations --><div class=\"col-no-break wd-jnl-aas-keywords\">\n\t\t<div class=\"replica-h4\">Keywords</div>\n\t\t<p><a href=\"/searchaaskeyword?source=aas&amp;type=kwd_group&amp;code=&amp;code_desc=galaxies%3A+evolution\">galaxies: evolution</a>; <a href=\"/searchaaskeyword?source=aas&amp;type=kwd_group&amp;code=&amp;code_desc=galaxies%3A+high-redshift\">galaxies: high-redshift</a>; <a href=\"/searchaaskeyword?source=aas&amp;type=kwd_group&amp;code=&amp;code_desc=galaxies%3A+star+formation\">galaxies: star formation</a>; <a href=\"/searchaaskeyword?source=aas&amp;type=kwd_group&amp;code=&amp;code_desc=gravitational+lensing%3A+strong\">gravitational lensing: strong</a>; <a href=\"/searchaaskeyword?source=aas&amp;type=kwd_group&amp;code=&amp;code_desc=infrared%3A+galaxies\">infrared: galaxies</a>; <a href=\"/searchaaskeyword?source=aas&amp;type=kwd_group&amp;code=&amp;code_desc=dust%2C+extinction\">dust, extinction</a></p>\n\t</div>\n<!-- Start Reprint Link -->\n\t<!-- End Reprint Link --><!-- <span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F838%2F2%2F137\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/838/2/137</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n            <p>We directly detect dust emission in an optically detected, multiply imaged galaxy lensed by the Frontier Fields cluster MACSJ0717.5+3745. We detect two images of the same galaxy at 1.1 mm with the AzTEC camera on the Large Millimeter Telescope leaving no ambiguity in the counterpart identification. This galaxy, MACS0717_Az9, is at <i>z</i>\u00a0&gt;\u00a04 and the strong lensing model (<i>\u03bc</i> = 7.5) allows us to calculate an intrinsic IR luminosity of 9.7\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>10</sup> <i>L</i><sub>\u2299</sub> and an obscured star formation rate of 14.6\u00a0\u00b1\u00a04.5 <i>M</i><sub>\u2299</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup>. The unobscured star formation rate from the UV is only 4.1\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.3 <i>M</i><sub>\u2299</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup>, which means the total star formation rate (18.7 \u00b1 4.5 <i>M</i><sub>\u2299</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup>) is dominated (75%\u201380%) by the obscured component. With an intrinsic stellar mass of only 6.9\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>9</sup> <i>M</i><sub>\u2299</sub>, MACS0717_Az9 is one of only a handful of <i>z</i>\u00a0&gt;\u00a04 galaxies at these lower masses that is detected in dust emission. This galaxy lies close to the estimated star formation sequence at this epoch. However, it does not lie on the dust obscuration relation (IRX-<i>\u03b2</i>) for local starburst galaxies and is instead consistent with the Small Magellanic Cloud attenuation law. This remarkable lower mass galaxy, showing signs of both low metallicity and high dust content, may challenge our picture of dust production in the early universe.</p></div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a title=\"Export BibTex\">BibTeX</a>\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=0004-637X/838/2/137&doi=10.3847/1538-4357/aa6573&exportFormat=iopexport_ris&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-ris\" title=\"Export RIS\">RIS</a>\n\t\t</span>\n\t</p>\n<!-- End Export citation and abstract tools -->\n<div class=\"linked-articles linked-articles--issue-nav\">\n\t<div class=\"linked-articles__wrapper\">\n\t\t<div class=\"linked-articles__prev\">\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/article/10.3847/1538-4357/aa65d2\"\n\t\t\t   class=\"linked-articles__btn event_read-more-articles\" data-event-action=\"Previous Article\"><span class=\"fa-icon\"><svg\n\t\t\t\t\taria-hidden=\"true\" focusable=\"false\" xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/2000/svg\" viewBox=\"0 0 192 class=\"linked-articles__btn event_read-more-articles\" data-event-action=\"Next Article\"><b>Next</b> article in issue<span\n\t\t\t\t\tclass=\"fa-icon fa-icon--right\"><svg aria-hidden=\"true\" focusable=\"false\"\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\txmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/2000/svg\" viewBox=\"0 0 192 512\"><path\n\t\t\t\t\td=\"M0 384.662V127.338c0-17.818 21.543-26.741 34.142-14.142l128.662 class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon bdt-1 mt-2 mb-2 wd-jnl-art-article-aas-related-links\">\n  <div class=\"replica-h3 mt-0\">\n    <a href=\"#\" class=\"reveal-trigger\" data-reveal-label-alt=\"Hide related links\">Related links</a>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"reveal-content\">\n    <div class=\"article-meta\"> \n      <ul>\n      \t<li><a href=\"https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2017ApJ...838..137P\" target=\"_blank\">NASA ADS Record <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://nedwww.ipac.caltech.edu/cgi-bin/nph-objsearch?refcode=2017ApJ...838..137P&search_type=Search \" target=\"_blank\">NED Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://simbad.u-strasbg.fr/simbad/sim-ref?querymethod=bib&simbo=on&bibcode=2017ApJ...838..137P\" target=\"_blank\">Simbad Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"/journal/0004-637X/page/About%20Related%20Links\" target=\"_blank\">About Related Links</a></li>\n      \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa6573s1\" name=\"apjaa6573s1\">1.\u00a0Introduction</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Over the past 20 years, surveys at rest-frame UV wavelengths have mapped out the history of unobscured star formation from the present day back to <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a08 (Madau &amp; Dickinson <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib34\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib34\">2014</a>). However, the roughly equal brightness of the cosmic infrared and optical backgrounds informs us that half the light from the formation and evolution of galaxies is obscured by dust (Lagache et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib31\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib31\">2005</a>). Surveys with the <em>Spitzer Space Telescope</em> and the <em>Herschel Space Observatory</em> showed that the contribution from infrared-luminous galaxies to the star formation rate density increases dramatically from <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00\u20132 (e.g., Caputi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib11\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib11\">2007</a>; Murphy et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib37\">2011a</a>; Magnelli et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib35\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib35\">2013</a>). Beyond <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a03, our census of the dust-obscured, and hence <em>total</em>, star formation activity is severely incomplete.</p><p>Until recently, surveys of dust-obscured activity at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a03 detected only the bright ultra-luminous infrared galaxies (ULIRGs, <em>L</em><sub>IR</sub> &gt; 10<sup>12</sup> <em>L</em><sub>\u2299</sub>, Casey et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib12\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib12\">2014</a>). While ULIRGs are prevalent at high redshift and many are not extreme starbursts like their local counterparts, they are not responsible for creating the bulk of the stars in the universe (Lagache et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib31\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib31\">2005</a>). At <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a02, much of the cosmic star formation activity is occurring in galaxies with <em>L</em><sub>IR</sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a010<sup>12</sup> <em>L</em><sub>\u2299</sub> (Murphy et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib37\">2011a</a>; Magnelli et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib35\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib35\">2013</a>). While these normal<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa6573fn1\">18</a></sup>\n galaxies can be selected at UV wavelengths, we have yet to directly detect most of their star formation activity as it is obscured by dust. The UV slope can provide an estimate of the dust extinction in the local universe (Meurer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib36\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib36\">1999</a>); however, this correction is uncertain at high redshift, where star formation is clumpy (Guo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib18\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib18\">2015</a>) and gas and dust are more widely distributed across the galaxy (e.g., Ivison et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib23\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib23\">2011</a>).</p><p>With its exceptional sensitivity, ALMA can directly detect dust in normal galaxies out to and beyond <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a03. In ALMA Cycles 0\u20132, several programs have pushed below the ULIRG limit, detecting dust in half a dozen UV-selected galaxies from <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a04\u20137.5 (e.g., Capak et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib10\">2015</a>; Watson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib59\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib59\">2015</a>; Willott et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib60\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib60\">2015</a>; Dunlop et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib16\">2017</a>). These studies show mixed results, with some sources having significant dust emission while others remain undetected (e.g., Schaerer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib48\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib48\">2015</a>; Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib3\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib3\">2016</a>).</p><p>A complementary facility for directly detecting dust in <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a04 galaxies is the Large Millimeter Telescope Alfonso Serrano (LMT, Hughes et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib21\">2010</a>). With a large aperture and fast mapping capability, the AzTEC camera (Wilson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib61\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib61\">2008</a>) on the 32 m LMT can survey dust in galaxies down to <em>L</em><sub>IR</sub>\u00a0\u223c\u00a06\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>11</sup> <em>L</em><sub>\u2299</sub> regardless of redshift due to the negative <em>K</em>-correction. Gravitational lensing can be used to push even deeper. In this paper, we present the direct detection of dust in a multiply imaged normal galaxy at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a04 with AzTEC on LMT.</p><p>Throughout this paper we assume a cosmology with <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a070 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>, \u03a9<sub>M</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.3, and \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.7.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa6573s2\" name=\"apjaa6573s2\">2.\u00a0Data</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjaa6573s2-1\" name=\"apjaa6573s2-1\">2.1.\u00a0Frontier Fields (FFs) Program</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The FFs program<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa6573fn2\">19</a></sup>\n started as a large <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> (<em>HST</em>) survey of low redshift clusters in order to identify and study high-redshift background galaxies that are gravitationally lensed. In this paper, we use the 13-band <em>HST</em> data, the <em>Spitzer</em>-IRAC imaging from 3.6 to 8 <em>\u03bc</em>m, and <em>K</em>-band imaging from Keck-MOSFIRE (program N097M and N135M, PI: Marchesini, Brammer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib5\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib5\">2016</a>). The <em>HST</em> data include the F435W, F606W, F814W, F105W, F125W, F140W, and F160W images from the FF program; the F475W, F625W, F775W, and F850LP images from CLASH (Postman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib43\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib43\">2012</a>); and the F275W and F336W images from the program GO-13389 (PI: Siana). The v2.1 UV-to-IRAC multi-wavelength photometric catalog used in this paper was constructed following Skelton et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib53\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib53\">2014</a>). The final catalog construction accounting for the intra-cluster light and contamination from brightest cluster galaxies will be described in H. Shipley et al. (2017, in preparation)</p><p>Since our target is a multiply imaged, strongly lensed galaxy, interpretation of its intrinsic properties will depend on the lensing model. STScI has released magnification maps as a function of background galaxy redshift for all FF clusters calculated from several independent lensing models.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa6573fn3\">20</a></sup>\n In this paper, we use the updated lensing models from Limousin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib33\">2016</a>) and Diego et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib15\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib15\">2015</a>), and we verified that our results are robust with other lensing models from STScI (Johnson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib24\">2014</a>; Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib63\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib63\">2015</a>). We present our results for two different lensing models to give a sense of how the parameters we are interested in (stellar mass, star formation rate, UV slope) change under different lensing models.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa6573s2-2\" name=\"apjaa6573s2-2\">2.2.\u00a0New AzTEC/LMT Observations</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>In 2014 November and December, we imaged the FF cluster MACSJ0717.5+3745 with AzTEC during early science with the LMT. During early science operations, we used the inner 32 m of the eventual 50 m aperture.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa6573fn4\">21</a></sup>\n AzTEC is a 1.1 mm bolometer array camera, with a beam size of 8.5 arcsec (FWHM) on the 32 m LMT. Data were taken in good weather conditions (<em>\u03c4</em><sub>225 GHz</sub> = 0.05\u20130.12). The on-source integration time was 21.1 hr. Our map covers 25 sq.\u00a0arcmin field reaching a mean rms of 0.24 mJy (central rms is 0.19 mJy).</p><p>The calibration and analysis of the AzTEC data follow the procedure described in Wilson et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib61\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib61\">2008</a>) and Scott et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib50\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib50\">2008</a>). The results on the number counts and source properties from the full LMT FF program<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa6573fn5\">22</a></sup>\n will be presented in future papers. Here we focus on a unique and rare source detected in our AzTEC map (MACS0717_Az9), which is coincident with an optically detected, multiply imaged lensed galaxy (known as 5.1/5.2/5.3, Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib62\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib62\">2009</a>). This is the only strongly lensed, multiply imaged system detected in our AzTEC survey. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa6573f1\">1</a> left shows AzTEC contours on the <em>HST</em> F160W image; two optical images (labeled 5.2 and 5.1) of the known multiply imaged system are detected with AzTEC (3.7 and 3.3<em>\u03c3</em> respectively). In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa6573s3-1\">3.1</a>, we demonstrate that at least half the millimeter flux detected by AzTEC must be associated with this system. A third &gt;3<em>\u03c3</em> AzTEC detection is visible in the top left corner of the left side of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa6573f1\">1</a>, but it is unassociated with the multiply imaged system that is the focus of this paper.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa6573f1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa6573f1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573f1_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=lhxlRsga%2BR6eLtHv5s13qxlOm7M%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573f1_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=lhxlRsga%2BR6eLtHv5s13qxlOm7M%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0<em>HST</em> F160W image toward MACSJ0717 with AzTEC/LMT contours (3 and 3.5<em>\u03c3</em>) in red. Our AzTEC map covers a wider region than shown here. The 3<em>\u03c3</em> detection in the top left is unrelated to the multiply imaged galaxy. We show the two multiply imaged systems: 5.1/5.2/5.3 at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a04 (blue circles) and 12.1/12.2/12.3 at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a01.71 (green squares). The right panel shows a zoom-in of the millimeter detection MACS0717_Az9, which is at the bottom right of the left panel. The size of the image is roughly equal to the FWHM of the beam.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa6573f1\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573f1_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=lhxlRsga%2BR6eLtHv5s13qxlOm7M%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa6573f1\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/838/2/137/1/apjaa6573f1_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843505&amp;Signature=lwPqTvfa70x711XvF2zUU6U6aec%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apjaa6573s2-3\" name=\"apjaa6573s2-3\">2.3.\u00a0Robustness of Millimeter Detections</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>In order to test the robustness of MACS0717_Az9 both as a millimeter source and as the counterpart to the <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a04 multiply imaged galaxy, we perform several simulations. We stress that since we have prior information on the positions of a known multiply imaged galaxy, we have more confidence in lower signal-to-noise detections.</p><p>First we determine the chance that our millimeter detections (3.7 and 3.3<em>\u03c3</em>) of the multiple-images 5.2 and 5.1 are spurious. We perform source extraction on 3000 noise maps. As with our original source list, we limit source extraction to regions of the map with noise &lt;0.4 mJy. We detect an average of 1 and 4 random &gt;3.7<em>\u03c3</em> and &gt;3.3<em>\u03c3</em> sources, respectively, in the noise maps. However, the sources we are interested in are not at random positions and specifically we detect two components of a previously known multiply imaged system. With this prior information, we find the chance of randomly detecting a\u00a0&gt;\u00a03.7<em>\u03c3</em> source within 1 AzTEC beam of 5.2 and a\u00a0&gt;\u00a03<em>\u03c3</em> source within 1 AzTEC beam of 5.1 is &lt;0.03%. We find the same answer if we vary the position of 5.2 and 5.1 on the map but conserve their relative separation. Therefore, the probability that our millimeter detection of this multiply imaged system (5.1 and 5.2) is a spurious detection is negligible.</p><p>Second, we test the chance that we should detect a multiply imaged source given the known lensing models and a model for a background population of millimeter sources. We develop 500 simulated maps using the empirical galaxy evolution model of B\u00e9thermin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib1\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib1\">2012</a>) for the input background millimeter galaxies and the lensing models of MACSJ0717 from the CATS group (Limousin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib33\">2016</a>). Here we report results using the <em>cored</em> mass model, and verified that there are no measurable differences with the <em>non-cored</em> mass model. We randomly populate simulated maps with millimeter sources down to intrinsic (e.g., non-lensed) 1.1 mm fluxes of \u22650.01 mJy. Redshifts are assigned to each millimeter source from the B\u00e9thermin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib1\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib1\">2012</a>) model. Then we run the background population through the cluster using the mass model in LENSTOOL (Kneib et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib27\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib27\">1996</a>; Jullo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib25\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib25\">2007</a>) to find the observed population of millimeter sources and ask how often the millimeter sources are multiply imaged. With no observed flux limit, multiple-image systems are found in all (99.4%) of our simulated maps, with an average of 6\u20137 systems per map. When we impose an observed flux limit of 0.7 mJy (i.e., &gt;3.5<em>\u03c3</em>), we find that 30% of these multiple systems have at least one image detectable in our simulated maps. Coupled with our estimated completeness limit of 50% at this low flux level (A. Monta\u00f1a et al. 2017, in preparation), our simulations predict that we will detect one multiply imaged system in our AzTEC map of MACS0717. Besides MACS0717_Az9, there are no other known multiply imaged sources in MACS0717 (using catalogs of known multiply imaged systems, e.g., Limousin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa6573bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjaa6573bib33\">2016</a>) that are individually detected in our AzTEC maps. We take the full list of multiply imaged sources including their magnification values and we stack the intrinsic millimeter flux for each multiply imaged source. We do not find that any other systems are detected, even when averaging the individual components in this way. Furthermore, none of the other AzTEC detections are in regions of strong magnification. Therefore, our simulations predict that we should detect one multiply imaged system like MACS0717_Az9.</p><p>Finally, we can further test the robustness of this millimeter detection by showing that the millimeter fluxes that we measure for 5.1, 5.2, and 5.3 are consistent with each other given their known magnifications (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa6573s3-1\">3.1</a>). The results of all three of these simulations and tests show that we have unambiguously detected dust emission in this multiply imaged system.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa6573s3\" name=\"apjaa6573s3\">3.\u00a0Analysis</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjaa6573s3-1\" name=\"apjaa6573s3-1\">3.1.\u00a0Counterpart of MACS0717_Az9</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Before we can discuss the nature of this millimeter source, we need to demonstrate that the multiply imaged system is the correct optical counterpart. From our simulations (A. Monta\u00f1a et al. 2017, in preparation), we find a positional accuracy for this system of 3.1 arcsec with 90% confidence. Within the search radius of MACS0717_Az9, we find two multiply imaged systems (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa6573f1\">1</a> right): 5.2 (<em>z</em> &gt; 4) and 12.2 (<em>z</em> = 1.71). But our AzTEC map also covers the other multiple images of these systems: 5.1/5.3 and 12.1/12.3 (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa6573f1\">1</a> left). We detect 5.1 in our AzTEC map at 3.3<em>\u03c3</em>. Source 5.3 has a lower magnification; as a result, the measured AzTEC flux is only 1.4<em>\u03c3</em> (Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa6573t1\">1</a>). Both 12.1 and 12.3 are undetected in our AzTEC map. In this Section, we show that the counterpart to MACS0717_Az9 must be 5.2.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjaa6573t1\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table
